###begin article-title 0
Distinct Type of Transmission Barrier Revealed by Study of Multiple Prion Determinants of Rnq1
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
Conceived and designed the experiments: MLK ILD. Performed the experiments: MLK GA ILD. Analyzed the data: MLK GA ILD. Contributed reagents/materials/analysis tools: ILD. Wrote the paper: ILD.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 355 356 355 356 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 352 356 352 356 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 436 437 436 437 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 433 437 433 437 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 741 749 741 749 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 771 772 771 772 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 768 772 768 772 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 786 793 786 793 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vivo</italic>
###xml 822 823 822 823 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 819 823 819 823 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 340 345 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 497 502 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Prions are self-propagating protein conformations. Transmission of the prion state between non-identical proteins, e.g. between homologous proteins from different species, is frequently inefficient. Transmission barriers are attributed to sequence differences in prion proteins, but their underlying mechanisms are not clear. Here we use a yeast Rnq1/[PIN+]-based experimental system to explore the nature of transmission barriers. [PIN+], the prion form of Rnq1, is common in wild and laboratory yeast strains, where it facilitates the appearance of other prions. Rnq1's prion domain carries four discrete QN-rich regions. We start by showing that Rnq1 encompasses multiple prion determinants that can independently drive amyloid formation in vitro and transmit the [PIN+] prion state in vivo. Subsequent analysis of [PIN+] transmission between Rnq1 fragments with different sets of prion determinants established that (i) one common QN-rich region is required and usually sufficient for the transmission; (ii) despite identical sequences of the common QNs, such transmissions are impeded by barriers of different strength. Existence of transmission barriers in the absence of amino acid mismatches in transmitting regions indicates that in complex prion domains multiple prion determinants act cooperatively to attain the final prion conformation, and reveals transmission barriers determined by this cooperative fold.
###end p 2
###begin title 3
Author Summary
###end title 3
###begin p 4
###xml 842 843 842 843 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 839 843 839 843 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 268 274 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">humans</span>
Prions, self-propagating protein conformations and causative agents of lethal neurodegenerative diseases, present a serious public health threat: they can arise sporadically and then spread by transmission to the same, as well as other, species. The risk of infecting humans with prions originating in wild and domestic animals is determined by the so-called transmission barriers. These barriers are attributed to differences in prion proteins from different species, but their underlying mechanisms are not clear. Recent findings that the prion state is transmitted through the interaction between short transmitting regions within prion domains revealed one type of transmission barrier, where productive templating is impeded by non-matching amino acids within transmitting regions. Here we present studies of the prion domain of the [PIN+]-forming protein, Rnq1, and describe a distinct type of transmission barrier not involving individual amino acid mismatches in the transmitting regions. Rnq1's prion domain is complex and encompasses four regions that can independently transmit the prion state. Our data suggest that multiple prion determinants of a complex prion domain act cooperatively to attain the prion conformation, and transmission barriers occur between protein variants that cannot form the same higher order structure, despite the identity of the region(s) driving the transmission.
###end p 4
###begin title 5
Introduction
###end title 5
###begin p 6
###xml 454 457 448 451 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Chiti1">[1]</xref>
###xml 458 461 452 455 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Eisenberg1">[2]</xref>
An increasing number of proteins has been found to form beta-sheet-rich aggregates called amyloid. In amyloid fibers, individual protein molecules are stacked on top of each other through the formation of inter-molecular beta-strands perpendicular to the fiber axis. Existing fibers template the conformational conversion of the protein molecules with the same amino acid sequence, making amyloid an aggregate-based self-propagating protein conformation [1],[2].
###end p 6
###begin p 7
###xml 219 222 219 222 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Eisenberg1">[2]</xref>
###xml 372 375 372 375 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Barnhart1">[3]</xref>
###xml 376 379 376 379 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Fowler1">[5]</xref>
###xml 572 574 572 574 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Sc</sup>
###xml 639 641 639 641 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Sc</sup>
###xml 696 699 696 699 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Collinge1">[6]</xref>
###xml 700 703 700 703 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Prusiner1">[7]</xref>
###xml 859 862 859 862 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Soto1">[8]</xref>
###xml 863 866 863 866 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Walker1">[9]</xref>
###xml 365 371 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">humans</span>
Intracellular aggregates or extracellular amyloid deposits are hallmarks of over 30 hereditary and sporadic disorders including Creutzfeldt-Jacob, Alzheimer's, Parkinson's and Huntington's diseases and type II diabetes [2]. Several lines of evidence also suggest that the amyloid state has been functionally harnessed by organisms as diverse as bacteria, fungi and humans [3]-[5]. Discovery of infectivity of Creutzfeldt-Jacob disease and other spongiform encephalopathies, and linking the infectivity to the presence of the aggregated conformation of the PrP protein, PrPSc, singled out these diseases as particularly hazardous, while PrPSc was termed "proteinaceous infectious agent", or prion [6],[7]. However, recent studies that revealed the inherent transmissibility of several mammalian amyloidoses blurred the border between prions and other amyloids [8],[9].
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 3 27 3 27 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 59 60 59 60 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 56 60 56 60 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 64 68 64 68 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URE3</italic>
###xml 78 79 78 79 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 75 79 75 79 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 169 173 169 173 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Wickner1">[10]</xref>
###xml 268 272 268 272 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Wickner2">[11]</xref>
###xml 372 380 372 380 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 415 419 415 419 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Tanaka1">[12]</xref>
###xml 420 424 420 424 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Patel1">[15]</xref>
###xml 465 466 465 466 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 462 466 462 466 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SWI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 548 552 548 552 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Du1">[16]</xref>
###xml 677 681 677 681 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Wickner2">[11]</xref>
###xml 683 687 683 687 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Du1">[16]</xref>
###xml 688 692 688 692 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch2">[19]</xref>
###xml 841 842 841 842 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 836 842 836 842 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PRION<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 981 982 981 982 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 978 982 978 982 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 986 990 986 990 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URE3</italic>
###xml 1000 1001 1000 1001 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 997 1001 997 1001 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SWI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1229 1230 1229 1230 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1226 1230 1226 1230 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1282 1286 1282 1286 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Firoozan1">[20]</xref>
###xml 1346 1350 1346 1350 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Cox1">[21]</xref>
###xml 1487 1491 1487 1491 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Wickner1">[10]</xref>
###xml 1493 1497 1493 1497 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-True1">[22]</xref>
###xml 1498 1502 1498 1502 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Talarek1">[30]</xref>
###xml 3 27 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 355 360 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 554 559 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
###xml 1360 1365 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1518 1523 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
In Saccharomyces cerevisiae, three epigenetic factors, [PSI+], [URE3] and [PIN+], manifest self-perpetuating amyloid conformations of Sup35, Ure2 and Rnq1, respectively [10]. The prion nature of these factors was established using genetic criteria proposed by Wickner [11], and the final proof of their protein-only transmission was obtained by infecting yeast cells with in vitro-made Sup35, Ure2 or Rnq1 amyloids [12]-[15]. Another recently discovered prion, [SWI+], satisfies the genetic criteria, but its amyloid nature has yet to be confirmed [16]. Yeast prions appear spontaneously or can be induced by transient overproduction of their respective prion-forming proteins [11], [16]-[19]. Once established, they are efficiently transmitted to daughter cells in mitosis and segregate in a non-Mendelian fashion in meiosis, and the [PRION+] state is maintained in the population until it is spontaneously lost or selectively eliminated. Phenotypes caused by the presence of [PSI+], [URE3] and [SWI+] are equivalent to loss-of-function mutations in, respectively, Sup35, Ure2 and Swi1, as these normally soluble proteins become sequestered into prion aggregates. For example, Sup35 is a translation termination factor, and [PSI+] increases the level of readthrough at stop codons [20], and can be detected as a suppressor of nonsense mutations [21]. Whether yeast prions are physiological epigentic modifiers of cellular functions, egoistic elements or diseases is a subject of debate [10], [22]-[30]. Nevertheless, yeast prions provide an excellent experimental model for addressing questions pertaining to self-propagating protein conformations.
###end p 8
###begin p 9
###xml 295 299 295 299 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch1">[18]</xref>
###xml 301 305 301 305 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Masison1">[31]</xref>
###xml 306 310 306 310 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Tessier1">[34]</xref>
###xml 427 431 427 431 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Tessier1">[34]</xref>
###xml 789 793 786 790 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Nelson1">[35]</xref>
The importance of direct templating in the transmission of the prion state is widely acknowledged, but the exact rules and determinants of this process remain unclear. Prion domains, which are terminally located in fungal prions, are essential and sufficient for prion formation and maintenance [18], [31]-[34]. A recent study implicates short sequences within prion domains as nucleation/recognition sites for prion formation [34]. In Sup35, which together with Ure2 and Rnq1 belongs to a class of proteins with QN-rich prion domains, recognition sites were mapped to two exceptionally QN-rich regions. A short peptide partially overlapping the primary N-terminal recognition site was previously shown to form in-register beta-sheets stabilized by hydrogen bonds between Q and N residues [35].
###end p 9
###begin p 10
###xml 186 189 186 189 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Collinge1">[6]</xref>
###xml 420 424 420 424 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Tessier1">[34]</xref>
###xml 425 429 425 429 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-DePace1">[36]</xref>
###xml 430 434 430 434 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Chen1">[37]</xref>
Differences in amino acid sequences within prion domains can lead to transmission barriers, which make conversion of homologous proteins from different species impossible or inefficient [6]. For Sup35, transmission barriers were observed even between closely related species and between wild type and mutant alleles of the same protein, and were explained by amino acid mismatches within the N-terminal recognition site [34],[36],[37].
###end p 10
###begin p 11
###xml 240 241 240 241 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 237 241 237 241 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 377 381 377 381 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-King2">[38]</xref>
###xml 382 386 382 386 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Shkundina1">[41]</xref>
###xml 458 462 458 462 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch1">[18]</xref>
###xml 464 468 464 468 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Bruce1">[42]</xref>
###xml 469 473 469 473 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Bradley1">[44]</xref>
###xml 638 642 638 642 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Hill1">[45]</xref>
###xml 643 647 643 647 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Tanaka2">[47]</xref>
###xml 654 662 654 662 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 760 764 760 764 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Bradley2">[48]</xref>
###xml 765 769 765 769 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Chang1">[51]</xref>
Yet, the prion phenomenon cannot be reduced to the stacking of short regions of the protein, as several lines of evidence underscore the importance of an overall conformation of the prion aggregate. For example, faithful propagation of [PSI+] involves parts of the Sup35 prion domain located outside of the presumptive recognition sites, e.g. a stretch of oligopeptide repeats [38]-[41]. Also, all prions exist as distinct heritable variants, called strains [18], [42]-[44]. Prion strains manifest distinct prion conformations of the same protein and, despite identical amino acid sequence, sometimes have different transmission barriers [45]-[47]. Both in vitro analysis and genetic data indicate that large regions of prion domains define strain differences [48]-[51].
###end p 11
###begin p 12
###xml 4 5 4 5 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1 5 1 5 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 107 108 107 108 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 104 108 104 108 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 115 119 115 119 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URE3</italic>
###xml 159 163 159 163 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Nakayashiki1">[24]</xref>
###xml 164 168 164 168 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Resende1">[26]</xref>
###xml 182 183 182 183 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 179 183 179 183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 298 302 298 302 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch3">[52]</xref>
###xml 329 330 329 330 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 326 330 326 330 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 338 339 338 339 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">P</underline>
###xml 338 339 338 339 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><underline>P</underline></italic>
###xml 338 339 338 339 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><underline>P</underline></italic></bold>
###xml 341 342 341 342 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 339 342 339 342 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 344 346 344 346 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in</underline>
###xml 344 346 344 346 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><underline>in</underline></bold>
###xml 378 385 378 385 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">de novo</italic>
###xml 403 404 403 404 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 400 404 400 404 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 406 410 406 410 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch2">[19]</xref>
###xml 411 415 411 415 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch4">[53]</xref>
###xml 416 420 416 420 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch5">[54]</xref>
###xml 520 524 520 524 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Bradley1">[44]</xref>
###xml 525 529 525 529 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Osherovich2">[55]</xref>
###xml 530 534 530 534 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Taneja1">[56]</xref>
###xml 571 572 571 572 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 568 572 568 572 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 628 632 628 632 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Derkatch6">[57]</xref>
###xml 633 637 633 637 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Vitrenko1">[58]</xref>
###xml 678 679 678 679 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 675 679 675 679 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 794 798 794 798 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Bradley3">[59]</xref>
###xml 799 803 799 803 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Meriin1">[60]</xref>
###xml 945 946 945 946 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 942 946 942 946 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 971 975 971 975 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Gokhale1">[61]</xref>
###xml 976 980 976 980 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Meriin2">[63]</xref>
###xml 1006 1007 1006 1007 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1003 1007 1003 1007 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1064 1068 1064 1068 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RNQ1</italic>
###xml 1092 1093 1092 1093 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1112 1122 1112 1118 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rnq1-&#916;</italic>
###xml 1174 1181 1170 1177 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">de novo</italic>
###xml 1200 1201 1196 1197 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1197 1201 1193 1197 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1206 1210 1202 1206 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ubc4</italic>
###xml 1219 1223 1215 1219 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Allen1">[64]</xref>
###xml 24 29 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 81 86 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
[PIN+] stands out among yeast prions as it is found in industrial and pathogenic yeast isolates, while [PSI+] and [URE3] are common only in laboratory strains [24],[26]. Several [PIN+]-associated phenotypes indicate its engagement in a broad spectrum of interactions with other prions and amyloids [52]. As its name implies, [PIN+] (for [PSI+]-inducibility) is required for the de novo formation of [PSI+] [19],[53],[54]. It also facilitates the appearance of various QN-rich and non-QN-rich prions and polyQ aggregates [44],[55],[56]. Current evidence suggests that [PIN+] promotes prion formation through direct cross-seeding [57],[58]. On the other hand, co-existence of [PIN+] and other prions or aggregating proteins may result in prion loss and in toxicity of polyQ-encompassing proteins [59],[60]. The mechanisms of prion incompatibility are not clear, but several studies show involvement of chaperones and endosomes/cytoskeleton in [PIN+]-related polyQ toxicity [61]-[63]. Consistent with all [PIN+] phenotypes being a gain of function, the disruption of RNQ1 does not make cells Pin+. On the contrary, rnq1-Delta interferes with increased frequency of spontaneous de novo appearance of [PSI+] in ubc4 mutants [64]. Since, despite the long-term efforts of several labs, no biological function could be assigned to the non-aggregated conformation of Rnq1, there is a possibility that ability to aggregate or to interact with QN-rich or otherwise aggregation-prone proteins is key to its function.
###end p 12
###begin p 13
###xml 69 70 69 70 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 66 70 66 70 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 176 183 176 183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">In vivo</italic>
###xml 189 196 189 196 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">132-405</sub>
###xml 214 215 214 215 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 211 215 211 215 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 226 233 226 233 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">153-405</sub>
###xml 294 295 294 295 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 291 295 291 295 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 310 314 310 314 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Sondheimer1">[33]</xref>
###xml 315 319 315 319 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Vitrenko2">[65]</xref>
###xml 321 329 321 329 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">In vitro</italic>
###xml 390 391 390 391 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 387 391 387 391 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pin<sup>&#8722;</sup></italic>
###xml 408 409 408 409 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 405 409 405 409 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 411 415 411 415 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Patel1">[15]</xref>
###xml 416 420 416 420 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Vitrenko1">[58]</xref>
###xml 421 425 421 425 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Wickner3">[66]</xref>
###xml 515 516 515 516 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 512 516 512 516 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 575 584 575 584 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000824-g001">Figure 1A</xref>
###xml 640 644 640 644 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Resende1">[26]</xref>
###xml 645 649 645 649 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000824-Vitrenko2">[65]</xref>
###xml 739 740 739 740 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 736 740 736 740 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 899 900 899 900 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 896 900 896 900 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1279 1280 1279 1280 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1276 1280 1276 1280 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
Lack of known function for soluble Rnq1 complicates defining the [PIN+] prion domain. The presumptive prion domain (aa 132-405 or 153-405) includes all Q- and QN-rich regions. In vivo, Rnq1132-405 can maintain [PIN+], and Rnq1153-405 can form a stable prion when fused to Sup35 lacking the [PSI+] prion domain [33],[65]. In vitro, these fragments form amyloid fibers, which can convert [pin-] cells into [PIN+] [15],[58],[66]. If these prion domain boundaries are correct, then the formation and maintenance of [PIN+] is driven by an extremely long and complex prion domain (Figure 1A; see below). This complexity has been noted previously [26],[65], however, no systematic analysis of the role of different structural determinants in [PIN+] formation and maintenance has been performed. Here we show that prion domain of Rnq1 carries multiple prion determinants that can independently maintain [PIN+]. Characterization of this complex prion domain allowed us to explore the role of individual prion determinants and overall prion fold in transmission of the prion state. We found that the ability to transmit the prion state is an intrinsic property of individual determinants/transmitting regions: one common region is required and generally sufficient for transmission of [PIN+] between Rnq1 fragments. However, efficiency of transmission between partially overlapping Rnq1 fragments is impeded by barriers due to differences in overall prion folds created by cooperative action of all prion determinants. This type of transmission barriers is clearly distinct from previously discussed barriers determined by aa mismatches in transmitting regions.
###end p 13
###begin title 14
###xml 46 47 46 47 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 43 47 43 47 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
No one QN region of Rnq1 is essential for [PIN+] maintenance.
###end title 14
###begin p 15

###xml 499 500 499 500 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>

###xml 538 542 534 538 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 550 554 546 550 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">RNQ1</italic>
###xml 572 575 568 571 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HIS</italic>
###xml 605 606 601 602 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 602 606 598 602 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 685 697 681 693 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">SUP35NM::YFP</italic>
###xml 803 804 799 800 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 800 804 796 800 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PSI<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1063 1064 1053 1054 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 1060 1064 1050 1054 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">PIN<sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 1173 1177 1163 1167 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">LEU2</italic>
###xml 1214 1215 1204 1205 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#8722;</sup>
###xml 1211 1215 1201 1205 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">pin<sup>&#8722;</sup></italic>
###xml 1238 1242 1228 1232 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 1371 1372 1357 1358 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">4</sub>
###xml 743 748 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 938 943 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
(A) Schematic diagram of Rnq1 and deletion constructs. QN-rich regions are in red (patterned blocks indicate oligopeptide repeats), QG10, is in orange, hydrophobic patches are in blue. Numeric intervals indicate aa boundaries of respective QN regions. For deletion constructs, lines indicate regions present; nomenclature refers to deleted regions. (B) Deletion of any one QN region does not lead to the loss of ability to maintain [PIN+]. Indicated LEU2-marked constructs were transformed into [PIN+][psi-] rnq1-Delta 74-D694 carrying a URA3-marked RNQ1 maintainer and a HIS-marked pGAL-SUP35NM::YFP [PSI+]-inducer. After selective elimination of the maintainer on FOA, expression of SUP35NM::YFP was transiently induced on SGal-Leu,His, and yeast were transferred to adeninelss media to score for [PSI+] ("from Gal"; shown is growth on SD-Ade after 10 days at 20degreesC) and to SD-Leu,His ("Growth Control"). In the control experiment yeast were grown on non-inducing SD-Leu,His instead of SGal-Leu,His ("from Glu"). (C) Rnq1 fragments introduced into the [PIN+] strain sustain the aggregated state after elimination of full-length Rnq1. Cultures carrying the indicated LEU2-marked plasmids were crossed to [pin-] 64-D697 carrying the URA3-marked pCUP-RNQ1::CFP. Diploids were selected on SD-Ura,Leu; reporter was induced by supplementing SD-Ura,Leu with 20microM CuSO4.
